## Chapter 4: Plagioclase Feldspar




### Subsection: 3.3b The role of granites in igneous rock series

Granites play a crucial role in the formation of igneous rock series. They are one of the most common types of felsic magmas and are known for their high silica content and viscosity. Granites are also known for their ability to undergo significant changes in composition and behavior as they cool and decompress.

#### The Formation of Granites

Granites are typically formed through the crystallization of molten rock, or magma, at the surface of the Earth. This magma is often derived from the melting of the Earth's mantle, but can also be formed through the melting of other types of rocks, such as sedimentary or metamorphic rocks. As the magma rises towards the surface, it cools and decompresses, leading to the formation of different minerals.

#### The Role of Granites in Igneous Rock Series

Granites are an important component of igneous rock series due to their ability to undergo significant changes in composition and behavior as they cool and decompress. This allows them to play a key role in the differentiation of magmas and the formation of different types of igneous rocks.

One of the key roles of granites in igneous rock series is their ability to assimilate surrounding rocks. As granites cool and decompress, they can incorporate surrounding rocks into their composition, leading to changes in their mineralogy and chemistry. This process can significantly alter the overall composition of the magma and can even lead to the formation of new types of igneous rocks.

Granites also play a crucial role in the formation of pegmatites, which are highly mineralized deposits of valuable minerals. As granites cool and decompress, they can form pegmatites through the crystallization of minerals such as quartz, feldspar, and mica. These pegmatites can contain high concentrations of valuable minerals, making them an important source of economic minerals.

#### The Significance of Granites in Igneous Petrology

The study of granites is essential in the field of igneous petrology. By understanding the formation and behavior of granites, we can gain valuable insights into the processes that occur during the crystallization of magmas and the formation of different types of igneous rocks.

Granites also play a crucial role in the study of the Earth's mantle. The composition and behavior of granites can provide clues about the composition and processes of the mantle, which is the layer of the Earth's interior between the crust and the core. By studying granites, we can gain a better understanding of the mantle and its role in the formation of igneous rocks.

In conclusion, granites are a key component of igneous rock series and play a crucial role in the formation of different types of igneous rocks. Their ability to undergo significant changes in composition and behavior as they cool and decompress makes them an essential topic of study in the field of igneous petrology. 





### Subsection: 3.4a Characteristics and formation of large silicic magmatic systems

Large silicic magmatic systems are a significant component of igneous rock series. These systems are characterized by their high silica content and their ability to produce a variety of different types of igneous rocks. In this section, we will explore the characteristics and formation of large silicic magmatic systems.

#### The Characteristics of Large Silicic Magmatic Systems

Large silicic magmatic systems are typically composed of felsic magmas, which have a silica content greater than 63%. These magmas are known for their high viscosity, which can range from 10<sup>8</sup> cP (10<sup>5</sup> Pa⋅s) for hot rhyolite magma at <cvt|1200|C||> to 10<sup>11</sup> cP (10<sup>8</sup> Pa⋅s) for cool rhyolite magma at <cvt|800|C||>. This high viscosity is due to the high silica content of these magmas, which leads to a high melting point and a thick, paste-like consistency.

Large silicic magmatic systems are also known for their ability to produce a variety of different types of igneous rocks. This is due to the fact that these systems can undergo significant changes in composition and behavior as they cool and decompress. This allows them to play a key role in the differentiation of magmas and the formation of different types of igneous rocks.

#### The Formation of Large Silicic Magmatic Systems

Large silicic magmatic systems are typically formed through the crystallization of molten rock, or magma, at the surface of the Earth. This magma is often derived from the melting of the Earth's mantle, but can also be formed through the melting of other types of rocks, such as sedimentary or metamorphic rocks. As the magma rises towards the surface, it cools and decompresses, leading to the formation of different minerals.

The formation of large silicic magmatic systems is also influenced by the presence of water and other volatile elements. The introduction of water and other volatiles can lower the melting point of the magma, leading to a decrease in viscosity and an increase in the formation of different types of igneous rocks.

#### The Role of Large Silicic Magmatic Systems in Igneous Rock Series

Large silicic magmatic systems play a crucial role in the formation of igneous rock series. Their ability to undergo significant changes in composition and behavior as they cool and decompress allows them to play a key role in the differentiation of magmas and the formation of different types of igneous rocks.

Furthermore, the presence of large silicic magmatic systems can also influence the formation of other types of igneous rocks. For example, the assimilation of surrounding rocks by granites, a common type of large silicic magmatic system, can lead to the formation of different types of igneous rocks. This process can significantly alter the overall composition of the magma and can even lead to the formation of new types of igneous rocks.

In conclusion, large silicic magmatic systems are a crucial component of igneous rock series. Their high silica content, high viscosity, and ability to undergo significant changes in composition and behavior make them a key player in the formation of different types of igneous rocks. Their presence can also influence the formation of other types of igneous rocks, making them a vital component in the study of igneous petrology.





### Subsection: 3.4b Petrogenesis and magma evolution in large silicic magmatic systems

The petrogenesis of large silicic magmatic systems is a complex process that involves the evolution of magmas as they move through the Earth's crust and mantle. This process is influenced by a variety of factors, including the composition of the source rock, the temperature and pressure conditions, and the presence of volatiles.

#### The Role of the Source Rock

The composition of the source rock plays a crucial role in the petrogenesis of large silicic magmatic systems. The source rock can be a variety of different types, including the Earth's mantle, sedimentary rocks, or metamorphic rocks. Each of these types of rocks has a different composition, which can influence the melting behavior and the resulting magma.

For example, the mantle is composed of a variety of different types of rocks, including peridotite and eclogite. Peridotite is a mafic rock with a high iron and magnesium content, while eclogite is a more evolved ultramafic rock with a higher silica content. The melting behavior of these rocks can vary significantly, leading to the formation of different types of magmas.

Sedimentary rocks, on the other hand, are composed of a variety of different types of minerals and can have a high silica content. The melting behavior of these rocks can be influenced by the presence of volatiles, which can lower the melting point and lead to the formation of more evolved magmas.

Metamorphic rocks, such as gneiss, can also play a role in the petrogenesis of large silicic magmatic systems. These rocks can undergo partial melting, leading to the formation of magmas with a variety of different compositions.

#### The Influence of Temperature and Pressure Conditions

The temperature and pressure conditions that a magma experiences as it moves through the Earth's crust and mantle can also influence its petrogenesis. As a magma moves towards the surface, it cools and decompresses, leading to changes in its composition. This can result in the formation of different minerals and the evolution of the magma towards a more evolved composition.

The temperature and pressure conditions can also influence the crystallization behavior of the magma. For example, at high temperatures and pressures, minerals such as olivine and pyroxene can crystallize, while at lower temperatures and pressures, minerals such as plagioclase and quartz can crystallize. This can lead to changes in the mineralogy and composition of the magma as it moves towards the surface.

#### The Role of Volatiles

Volatiles, such as water and carbon dioxide, can also play a significant role in the petrogenesis of large silicic magmatic systems. These volatiles can lower the melting point of the source rock, leading to the formation of more evolved magmas. They can also influence the crystallization behavior of the magma, leading to the formation of different minerals.

In addition, volatiles can also be responsible for the formation of hydrothermal alteration assemblages, which can significantly alter the composition of the magma. This can result in the formation of new minerals and the precipitation of elements, leading to changes in the overall composition of the magma.

#### The Evolution of Magmas

The evolution of magmas in large silicic magmatic systems is a complex process that involves changes in composition, mineralogy, and texture. As a magma moves towards the surface, it can undergo changes in temperature and pressure, leading to changes in its composition. This can result in the formation of different minerals and the precipitation of elements, leading to changes in the overall composition of the magma.

In addition, the interaction of magmas with the surrounding rocks can also influence their evolution. For example, the assimilation of surrounding rocks can lead to changes in the composition of the magma, while the mixing of different magmas can result in the formation of hybrid magmas with unique compositions.

The evolution of magmas can also be influenced by the presence of volatiles, which can lower the melting point and lead to the formation of more evolved magmas. The crystallization of different minerals can also result in changes in the composition of the magma, leading to the formation of different types of igneous rocks.

In conclusion, the petrogenesis and magma evolution in large silicic magmatic systems is a complex process that involves the interaction of various factors, including the composition of the source rock, temperature and pressure conditions, and the presence of volatiles. This process can result in the formation of a variety of different types of igneous rocks, each with its own unique characteristics. 





### Subsection: 3.5a Geology and igneous activity in Hawaii

The Hawaiian Islands are a chain of volcanic islands formed by a hot spot in the Earth's mantle. The Hawaiian hot spot is responsible for the creation of the Hawaiian-Emperor seamount chain, a series of volcanic structures that extends from the Hawaiian Islands to the Emperor Seamounts. The hot spot is located at a depth of about 100 km below the surface and has been active for at least 70 million years.

#### The Hawaiian Islands

The Hawaiian Islands are the result of the Hawaiian hot spot's activity. As the Pacific Plate moves over the hot spot, magma is generated and rises to the surface, creating new islands. The oldest islands in the chain, Kure and Midway, are located to the northwest of the main Hawaiian Islands. These islands are composed of oceanic crust and are not visible above sea level.

The main Hawaiian Islands, from oldest to youngest, are Hawaii, Kahoolawe, Maui, Lanai, Molokai, Oahu, and Kauai. These islands are all composed of shield volcanoes, which are large, low-angle volcanoes that are typically found on oceanic crust. The youngest island, Hawaii, is still actively volcanic, with lava flows from Kilauea volcano occasionally reaching the ocean.

#### The Hawaiian-Emperor Seamount Chain

The Hawaiian-Emperor seamount chain extends from the Hawaiian Islands to the Emperor Seamounts, a chain of underwater volcanoes located north of Hawaii. The chain is composed of both oceanic and continental crust, with the oldest parts of the chain being composed of continental crust.

The oldest part of the chain, the Hawaiian Islands, is composed of oceanic crust. As the Pacific Plate moves over the hot spot, the oceanic crust is created and the oldest parts of the chain are subducted beneath the younger parts. This process is known as seafloor spreading and is responsible for the creation of the Hawaiian-Emperor seamount chain.

#### The Role of the Hawaiian Hot Spot

The Hawaiian hot spot plays a crucial role in the petrogenesis of the Hawaiian Islands and the Hawaiian-Emperor seamount chain. The hot spot is responsible for the generation of magma, which is then extruded at the surface to create new landforms. The composition of the magma is influenced by the hot spot's melting behavior, which is in turn influenced by the composition of the source rock and the temperature and pressure conditions.

The Hawaiian hot spot also plays a role in the evolution of the Hawaiian Islands. As the hot spot continues to generate magma, the islands continue to grow and evolve. The oldest islands, such as Kure and Midway, are composed of older, more evolved magmas, while the younger islands, such as Hawaii, are composed of younger, more primitive magmas.

In conclusion, the Hawaiian hot spot is a crucial component of the Hawaiian Islands and the Hawaiian-Emperor seamount chain. Its activity continues to shape the islands and the seamount chain, and its influence can be seen in the composition and evolution of the landforms.

In this chapter, we have explored the significance of igneous rock series in understanding the evolution of the Earth's crust. We have learned that igneous rocks are formed through the cooling and solidification of molten rock, and that they play a crucial role in shaping the Earth's surface. We have also discussed the different types of igneous rocks, including plutonic and volcanic rocks, and how they are formed.

Furthermore, we have delved into the concept of rock series, which refers to a group of related igneous rocks that share similar chemical and mineralogical characteristics. We have seen how rock series can be used to trace the evolution of the Earth's crust, as different rock series are formed at different stages of the Earth's history. This understanding is crucial in the field of geology, as it allows us to reconstruct the past and predict future changes in the Earth's crust.

In conclusion, the study of igneous rock series is essential in understanding the dynamic nature of the Earth's crust. It provides us with valuable insights into the processes that shape our planet and helps us to better understand the world around us.

In this chapter, we will explore the topic of metamorphic rocks and their significance in the field of igneous petrology. Metamorphic rocks are formed when igneous rocks undergo changes in mineral composition and texture due to high temperatures and pressures. These changes can occur deep within the Earth's crust or in the presence of fluids, such as water or magma. The study of metamorphic rocks is crucial in understanding the evolution of the Earth's crust and the processes that shape it.

We will begin by discussing the different types of metamorphic rocks, including their formation and characteristics. This will include a detailed examination of the various factors that influence the metamorphism of igneous rocks, such as temperature, pressure, and fluid composition. We will also explore the different types of metamorphic processes, such as recrystallization, melting, and metamorphic differentiation.

Next, we will delve into the significance of metamorphic rocks in the field of igneous petrology. This will include a discussion on how metamorphic rocks can provide insights into the composition and evolution of the Earth's mantle and crust. We will also examine the role of metamorphic rocks in the formation of ore deposits and their economic importance.

Finally, we will explore some of the advanced techniques used in the study of metamorphic rocks, such as microstructural analysis and geochemical modeling. These techniques allow us to better understand the complex processes involved in metamorphism and their impact on the Earth's crust.

By the end of this chapter, readers will have a comprehensive understanding of metamorphic rocks and their significance in the field of igneous petrology. This knowledge will not only enhance their understanding of the Earth's crust and its evolution, but also provide a foundation for further exploration and research in this fascinating field.


## Chapter 4: Metamorphic Rocks:




### Subsection: 3.5b Geology and igneous activity in Iceland

Iceland, like Hawaii, is a volcanically active island formed by a hot spot in the Earth's mantle. The Iceland hot spot is located at a depth of about 100 km below the surface and has been active for at least 20 million years. The hot spot is responsible for the creation of the Mid-Atlantic Ridge, a series of underwater volcanic structures that extends from Iceland to the Azores.

#### The Icelandic Islands

The Icelandic Islands are the result of the Iceland hot spot's activity. As the North American Plate moves over the hot spot, magma is generated and rises to the surface, creating new islands. The oldest islands in the chain, Surtsey and Heimaey, are located to the southwest of the main Icelandic Islands. These islands are composed of oceanic crust and are not visible above sea level.

The main Icelandic Islands, from oldest to youngest, are Vestmannaeyjar, Snaefellsnes, Reykjanes, and the Mid-Atlantic Ridge. These islands are all composed of shield volcanoes, similar to the Hawaiian Islands. The youngest island, the Mid-Atlantic Ridge, is still actively volcanic, with lava flows from the ridge occasionally reaching the surface.

#### The Mid-Atlantic Ridge

The Mid-Atlantic Ridge extends from Iceland to the Azores and is a series of underwater volcanic structures. The ridge is composed of both oceanic and continental crust, with the oldest parts of the ridge being composed of continental crust. The ridge is also a divergent plate boundary, where the North American Plate and the Eurasian Plate are moving apart.

The oldest part of the ridge, the Mid-Atlantic Ridge, is composed of continental crust. As the North American Plate moves over the hot spot, the continental crust is created and the oldest parts of the ridge are subducted beneath the younger parts. This process is known as seafloor spreading and is responsible for the creation of the Mid-Atlantic Ridge.

#### The Role of the Iceland Hot Spot

The Iceland hot spot plays a crucial role in the geology and igneous activity of Iceland. The hot spot's activity is responsible for the creation of the Icelandic Islands and the Mid-Atlantic Ridge. The hot spot also influences the climate of Iceland, as the heat from the hot spot is responsible for the island's relatively mild climate compared to other locations at similar latitudes. The Iceland hot spot is a fascinating area of study for geologists and volcanologists, and its activity continues to shape the Earth's surface today.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the significance of igneous rock series in understanding the evolution of the Earth's crust. We have learned that igneous rocks are formed through the cooling and solidification of molten rock, and that they play a crucial role in shaping the Earth's surface. We have also discussed the different types of igneous rocks, including plutonic, volcanic, and extrusive igneous rocks, and how they are formed.

Furthermore, we have delved into the concept of rock series, which refers to a group of related igneous rocks that share similar mineral compositions and textures. We have seen how rock series can be used to trace the evolution of the Earth's crust, as different rock series are formed at different stages of the cooling and solidification process. This allows us to understand the history of the Earth's crust and how it has changed over time.

Overall, the study of igneous rock series is essential in the field of geology, as it provides valuable insights into the formation and evolution of the Earth's crust. By understanding the significance of igneous rock series, we can gain a deeper understanding of the processes that shape our planet and the role that igneous rocks play in these processes.

## Chapter 4: Metamorphic Rocks

 4.1: Metamorphic Processes

Metamorphic rocks are formed when igneous or sedimentary rocks are subjected to high temperatures and pressures, causing them to change in mineral composition and texture. This process is known as metamorphism and it plays a crucial role in the evolution of the Earth's crust. In this section, we will explore the different types of metamorphic processes and their significance in the geological history of our planet.

#### Subsection 4.1a: Metamorphic Processes and Their Significance

Metamorphic processes are classified based on the type of original rock and the conditions under which it undergoes metamorphism. The three main types of metamorphic processes are contact metamorphism, regional metamorphism, and dynamic metamorphism.

Contact metamorphism occurs when a rock is heated by a nearby intrusion of magma. This process is responsible for the formation of high-grade metamorphic rocks, such as hornfels and gneiss. Contact metamorphism is significant because it can provide insights into the composition and temperature of the intruding magma.

Regional metamorphism, on the other hand, occurs when a large area of rock is subjected to high temperatures and pressures. This process is responsible for the formation of low-grade metamorphic rocks, such as slate and phyllite. Regional metamorphism is significant because it can provide information about the tectonic history of the Earth's crust.

Dynamic metamorphism is a type of metamorphism that occurs in the presence of fluid. This process is responsible for the formation of high-grade metamorphic rocks, such as eclogite and greenschist. Dynamic metamorphism is significant because it can provide insights into the composition and movement of fluids in the Earth's crust.

The significance of metamorphic processes goes beyond their role in the formation of new rocks. They also play a crucial role in the cycling of elements and minerals in the Earth's crust. For example, the metamorphism of sedimentary rocks can release valuable minerals and elements that can be used in various industries.

Furthermore, metamorphic processes can also provide insights into the history of the Earth's crust. By studying the mineral composition and texture of metamorphic rocks, scientists can determine the conditions under which they were formed and the tectonic events that may have influenced their formation.

In conclusion, metamorphic processes are essential in the study of igneous petrology. They not only play a crucial role in the formation of new rocks but also provide valuable insights into the history and evolution of the Earth's crust. In the next section, we will explore the different types of metamorphic rocks and their formation processes in more detail.


# Advanced Igneous Petrology Textbook

## Chapter 4: Metamorphic Rocks

 4.1: Metamorphic Processes

Metamorphic rocks are formed when igneous or sedimentary rocks are subjected to high temperatures and pressures, causing them to change in mineral composition and texture. This process is known as metamorphism and it plays a crucial role in the evolution of the Earth's crust. In this section, we will explore the different types of metamorphic processes and their significance in the geological history of our planet.

#### Subsection 4.1a: Metamorphic Processes and Their Significance

Metamorphic processes are classified based on the type of original rock and the conditions under which it undergoes metamorphism. The three main types of metamorphic processes are contact metamorphism, regional metamorphism, and dynamic metamorphism.

Contact metamorphism occurs when a rock is heated by a nearby intrusion of magma. This process is responsible for the formation of high-grade metamorphic rocks, such as hornfels and gneiss. Contact metamorphism is significant because it can provide insights into the composition and temperature of the intruding magma.

Regional metamorphism, on the other hand, occurs when a large area of rock is subjected to high temperatures and pressures. This process is responsible for the formation of low-grade metamorphic rocks, such as slate and phyllite. Regional metamorphism is significant because it can provide information about the tectonic history of the Earth's crust.

Dynamic metamorphism is a type of metamorphism that occurs in the presence of fluid. This process is responsible for the formation of high-grade metamorphic rocks, such as eclogite and greenschist. Dynamic metamorphism is significant because it can provide insights into the composition and movement of fluids in the Earth's crust.

The significance of metamorphic processes goes beyond their role in the formation of new rocks. They also play a crucial role in the cycling of elements and minerals in the Earth's crust. For example, the metamorphism of sedimentary rocks can release valuable minerals and elements that can be used in various industries. Additionally, metamorphic processes can also provide insights into the tectonic history of the Earth's crust, as different types of metamorphism can occur in different geological settings.

### Subsection 4.1b: Metamorphic Processes and Their Effects on Rock Composition

Metamorphic processes have a significant impact on the composition of rocks. As rocks undergo metamorphism, their mineral composition and texture can change dramatically. This is due to the high temperatures and pressures that these rocks are subjected to, which can cause minerals to recrystallize and form new minerals.

Contact metamorphism, for example, can result in the formation of high-grade metamorphic rocks with a different mineral composition than the original rock. This is because the intruding magma can introduce new minerals and elements, which can alter the mineral composition of the surrounding rock.

Regional metamorphism, on the other hand, can result in the formation of low-grade metamorphic rocks with a different texture than the original rock. This is because the high temperatures and pressures can cause the minerals in the rock to recrystallize and form new minerals with a different texture.

Dynamic metamorphism can also have a significant impact on rock composition. The presence of fluid can cause minerals to dissolve and recrystallize, resulting in the formation of new minerals with a different composition than the original rock.

Overall, metamorphic processes play a crucial role in the evolution of the Earth's crust. They can alter the mineral composition and texture of rocks, providing valuable insights into the tectonic history of our planet.

Overall, metamorphic processes play a crucial role in the evolution of the Earth's crust and can provide valuable insights into the tectonic history of our planet. By studying the effects of these processes on rock composition, we can gain a better understanding of the complex processes that shape our planet.

## Chapter 4: Metamorphic Rocks




### Subsection: 3.6a Introduction to Continental Flood Basalts (CFBs)

Continental Flood Basalts (CFBs) are a type of igneous rock that is formed through large-scale volcanic eruptions. These eruptions are typically associated with the formation of large, flat-lying lava flows that cover large areas of the Earth's surface. CFBs are found on all continents, but are most commonly associated with the mid-Miocene transition in the Columbia River Basalt Group (CRBG).

#### The Columbia River Basalt Group

The Columbia River Basalt Group (CRBG) is a large igneous province (LIP) located in the northwestern United States. It is composed of more than 300 individual basalt lava flows that have an average volume of . The CRBG is divided into four major categories: The Steens Basalt, Grande Ronde Basalt, the Wanapum Basalt, and the Saddle Mountains Basalt. The various lava flows have been dated by radiometric dating—particularly through measurement of the ratios of isotopes of potassium to argon.

The transition to flood volcanism in the CRBG, similar to other large igneous provinces, was also marked by atmospheric loading through the mass exsolution and emission of volatiles, via the process of volcanic degassing. Comparative analysis of volatile concentrations in source feeder dikes to associated extruded flow units have been quantitatively measured to determine the magnitude of degassing exhibited in CRBG eruptions. Of the more than 300 individual flows associated with the CRBG, the Roza flow contains some of the most well chemically preserved basalts for volatile analysis. Contained within the Wanapum formation, Roza is one of the most extensive members of the CRBG with an area of 40,300 square kilometres and a volume of 1,300 cubic kilometres. With magmatic volatile values assumed at 1 - 1.5 percent by weight concentration for source feeder dikes, the emission of sulphur for the Roza flow is calculated to be on the order of 12Gt (12,000 million tonnes) at a rate of 1.2Gt (1,200 million tonnes) annually, in the form of sulphur dioxide. However, other research through petrologic analysis has yielded SO2 mass degassing values at 0.12% - 0.28% of the total erupted mass of the magma, translating to lower emission estimates in the range of 0.2 - 0.6 Gt/yr.

#### The Role of CFBs in the Earth's Evolution

CFBs play a significant role in the Earth's evolution, particularly in the mid-Miocene transition. The large-scale volcanic eruptions associated with CFBs release large amounts of volatiles into the atmosphere, which can have a significant impact on the Earth's climate and atmosphere. The release of these volatiles can also lead to changes in the Earth's magnetic field, as seen in the Selli Event.

Furthermore, the formation of CFBs can also have a significant impact on the Earth's surface. The large, flat-lying lava flows associated with CFBs can cover large areas of the Earth's surface, altering the topography and creating new landforms. This can have implications for the distribution of life on Earth, as well as the movement of tectonic plates.

In conclusion, CFBs are a crucial component of the Earth's evolution, playing a significant role in shaping the Earth's surface, atmosphere, and climate. Further research into the formation and impact of CFBs will continue to enhance our understanding of the Earth's past and future.

## Chapter 4: Metamorphic Rocks




### Subsection: 3.6b Petrologic and geochemical characteristics of CFBs

Continental Flood Basalts (CFBs) are characterized by their large-scale, flat-lying lava flows that cover extensive areas of the Earth's surface. These basalts are typically formed through large-scale volcanic eruptions, and their petrologic and geochemical characteristics provide valuable insights into the processes of their formation and the evolution of the Earth's mantle.

#### Petrologic Characteristics of CFBs

The petrologic characteristics of CFBs are largely determined by their mineral composition. The most common minerals found in CFBs are plagioclase feldspar, pyroxene, and olivine. These minerals are typically found in varying proportions, depending on the specific CFB and the conditions under which it was formed.

The mineral composition of CFBs can be used to infer the conditions under which they were formed. For example, the presence of olivine suggests that the magma was formed at high temperatures and pressures, while the presence of plagioclase feldspar suggests that the magma was formed at lower temperatures and pressures.

#### Geochemical Characteristics of CFBs

The geochemical characteristics of CFBs are largely determined by their chemical composition. The most common elements found in CFBs are iron, magnesium, calcium, and silicon. These elements are typically found in varying proportions, depending on the specific CFB and the conditions under which it was formed.

The chemical composition of CFBs can be used to infer the composition of the mantle from which they were derived. For example, the presence of high iron and magnesium contents suggests that the mantle was enriched in these elements, while the presence of low iron and magnesium contents suggests that the mantle was depleted in these elements.

#### The Significance of CFBs

The petrologic and geochemical characteristics of CFBs have significant implications for our understanding of the Earth's mantle. The large-scale, flat-lying lava flows of CFBs suggest that the mantle is capable of generating large volumes of magma, and that this magma can be efficiently transported to the surface.

The mineral and chemical composition of CFBs provides valuable insights into the processes of mantle convection and the evolution of the Earth's mantle. For example, the presence of olivine suggests that the mantle is capable of generating high temperatures and pressures, while the presence of plagioclase feldspar suggests that the mantle is capable of generating lower temperatures and pressures.

In addition, the geochemical characteristics of CFBs can be used to trace the movement of the Earth's plates. For example, the presence of high iron and magnesium contents in CFBs can be used to trace the movement of the Pacific Plate, while the presence of low iron and magnesium contents can be used to trace the movement of the North American Plate.

In conclusion, the petrologic and geochemical characteristics of CFBs provide valuable insights into the processes of mantle convection and the evolution of the Earth's mantle. These insights are crucial for our understanding of the Earth's mantle and its role in the dynamics of the Earth's surface.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the significance of igneous rock series, exploring their formation, characteristics, and the role they play in the Earth's crust. We have learned that igneous rock series are not just random collections of rocks, but rather, they are a reflection of the complex processes that occur beneath the Earth's surface. 

We have also seen how the study of igneous rock series can provide valuable insights into the history of the Earth's crust, helping us to understand the evolution of the planet over time. By examining the different types of igneous rocks and their distribution, we can piece together a picture of the tectonic processes that have shaped the Earth's surface.

Furthermore, we have discussed the importance of understanding the significance of igneous rock series in various fields, including geology, geophysics, and environmental science. The knowledge gained from studying igneous rock series can be applied to a wide range of practical applications, from mineral exploration to hazard assessment and mitigation.

In conclusion, the study of igneous rock series is a crucial aspect of geology. It not only enhances our understanding of the Earth's crust but also provides a foundation for further research and application in various fields.

## Chapter 4: The Role of Igneous Processes in Orogenic Systems

### Introduction

The study of igneous processes is a crucial aspect of geology, particularly in the context of orogenic systems. Orogenic systems, or mountain-building processes, are fundamental to the evolution of the Earth's crust and the formation of its diverse landscapes. This chapter will delve into the role of igneous processes in these systems, exploring how they contribute to the formation of mountains and other geological features.

Igneous processes are responsible for the creation of many of the Earth's most striking geological features. They are involved in the formation of volcanic mountains, such as the Andes and the Himalayas, as well as in the creation of intrusive igneous complexes, such as the Skaergaard intrusion in Greenland. These processes are also integral to the formation of granitic gneiss, a common type of metamorphic rock found in many mountain ranges.

In this chapter, we will explore the various types of igneous processes that contribute to orogenic systems. These include volcanic processes, such as lava flow and explosive volcanism, as well as intrusive processes, such as plutonism and sill emplacement. We will also discuss the role of these processes in the formation of different types of igneous rocks, and how these rocks can be used to understand the history of orogenic systems.

Finally, we will examine the interplay between igneous processes and other geological processes, such as metamorphism and faulting, in the context of orogenic systems. This will provide a comprehensive understanding of how these processes work together to shape the Earth's crust and create the diverse landscapes we see today.

By the end of this chapter, readers should have a solid understanding of the role of igneous processes in orogenic systems, and be able to apply this knowledge to the study of various geological features and processes. This chapter will serve as a foundation for further exploration into the fascinating world of igneous petrology.




### Subsection: 3.7a Phase equilibria and mantle sources of CFBs

The phase equilibria of the mantle sources of Continental Flood Basalts (CFBs) play a crucial role in understanding the formation and evolution of these igneous rocks. The phase equilibria of a system describe the conditions under which different phases of a substance can coexist in equilibrium. In the context of CFBs, the phase equilibria of the mantle sources can provide insights into the conditions under which the magma was formed and the processes that led to the formation of CFBs.

#### Phase Equilibria of the Mantle Sources of CFBs

The mantle sources of CFBs are typically composed of olivine, pyroxene, and feldspar. These minerals are stable at different temperatures and pressures, and their relative proportions in the mantle source can be inferred from the phase equilibria of the system.

At high temperatures and pressures, olivine is the stable phase. As the temperature decreases or the pressure increases, olivine transforms into pyroxene. Finally, at lower temperatures and pressures, pyroxene transforms into feldspar. This sequence of phase transformations can be represented by the following equation:

$$
\text{Olivine} \leftrightarrow \text{Pyroxene} \leftrightarrow \text{Feldspar}
$$

The phase equilibria of this system can be represented on a phase diagram, where the temperature and pressure are plotted against the composition of the system. The phase diagram can provide insights into the conditions under which each phase is stable, and the conditions under which the system can be in equilibrium.

#### Implications of Phase Equilibria for CFB Formation

The phase equilibria of the mantle sources of CFBs have significant implications for the formation of these igneous rocks. The phase equilibria can provide insights into the temperature and pressure conditions under which the magma was formed. For example, if the phase equilibria indicate that the mantle source was at high temperatures and pressures, this suggests that the magma was formed through a process of mantle melting.

Furthermore, the phase equilibria can also provide insights into the composition of the mantle source. For example, if the phase equilibria indicate that the mantle source was enriched in olivine, this suggests that the mantle source was enriched in iron and magnesium. Conversely, if the phase equilibria indicate that the mantle source was enriched in feldspar, this suggests that the mantle source was enriched in calcium and silicon.

In conclusion, the phase equilibria of the mantle sources of CFBs play a crucial role in understanding the formation and evolution of these igneous rocks. By studying the phase equilibria, we can gain insights into the conditions under which the magma was formed and the processes that led to the formation of CFBs.




### Subsection: 3.7b Constraints on CFB mantle sources using petrologic and geochemical data

The petrologic and geochemical data of Continental Flood Basalts (CFBs) can provide valuable insights into the nature of their mantle sources. These data can be used to constrain the composition and evolution of the mantle sources, and to understand the processes that led to the formation of CFBs.

#### Petrologic Data

The petrologic data of CFBs can be used to infer the composition of their mantle sources. The mineral assemblage of CFBs, for example, can provide insights into the temperature and pressure conditions under which the magma was formed. As discussed in the previous section, the phase equilibria of the mantle sources can be represented on a phase diagram, where the temperature and pressure are plotted against the composition of the system. The phase diagram can provide insights into the conditions under which each phase is stable, and the conditions under which the system can be in equilibrium.

#### Geochemical Data

The geochemical data of CFBs can be used to infer the evolution of their mantle sources. The geochemical composition of CFBs can change over time due to processes such as fractional melting and differentiation. Fractional melting occurs when a magma is melted in stages, with different minerals melting at different temperatures. This process can result in the separation of different minerals, each with its own geochemical composition. Differentiation, on the other hand, occurs when a magma separates into layers of different densities. This process can result in the stratification of the magma, with different layers having different geochemical compositions.

#### Implications of Petrologic and Geochemical Data for CFB Formation

The petrologic and geochemical data of CFBs can provide valuable insights into the formation of these igneous rocks. The data can be used to infer the composition and evolution of the mantle sources, and to understand the processes that led to the formation of CFBs. For example, the petrologic and geochemical data can be used to infer the temperature and pressure conditions under which the magma was formed, and the processes that led to the formation of different minerals. The data can also be used to infer the evolution of the mantle sources, and the processes that led to the formation of different layers within the magma.




### Subsection: 3.8a Geology and petrogenesis of Bushveld, Stillwater, and Skaergaard intrusions

The Bushveld, Stillwater, and Skaergaard intrusions are three of the most significant layered igneous complexes in the world. They are known for their unique geological and petrological characteristics, as well as their economic importance due to the presence of valuable mineral deposits.

#### Bushveld Igneous Complex

The Bushveld Igneous Complex, located in South Africa, is one of the largest layered intrusions in the world. It is divided into an eastern and western lobe, with a further northern extension. The complex was formed about 2 billion years ago, and it is thought to predate the nearby Vredefort impact by some 30 million years.

The formation of the Bushveld Complex is associated with the injection of molten rock from Earth's mantle through long vertical cracks in the Earth's crust. This resulted in the formation of a structure similar to a layered cake, with distinct rock strata. The complex includes three PGM-bearing layers, referred to as reefs, which are of particular interest due to their economic importance.

The extrusions were emplaced over an early diabasic sill, outcrops of which are visible on the southeastern side of the Complex. These are typically greenish in colour and composed of clinopyroxene, altered to hornblende and plagioclase, and are regarded as the earliest phase of the Complex.

The Bushveld Complex varies in thickness, reaching up to 10 km in places. Lithologies vary from largely ultramafic peridotite, chromitite, harzburgite, and bronzitite in the lower sections to mafic norite, anorthosite, and gabbro toward the top. The complex also includes the felsic Rustenburg Layered Suite and the Lebowa Granite Suite.

#### Stillwater Igneous Complex

The Stillwater Igneous Complex, located in Montana, USA, is another significant layered intrusion. It is known for its economic importance due to the presence of valuable mineral deposits, including platinum, palladium, and copper.

The formation of the Stillwater Complex is associated with the emplacement of a series of mafic to felsic intrusive igneous rocks. These rocks are thought to have been derived from the melting of the mantle and the surrounding crust, and they are characterized by their layered structure.

#### Skaergaard Intrusion

The Skaergaard Intrusion, located in Greenland, is a layered igneous complex that is known for its unique petrological characteristics. It is composed of a series of layered mafic to felsic intrusive igneous rocks, which are thought to have been derived from the melting of the mantle and the surrounding crust.

The Skaergaard Intrusion is particularly interesting due to its layered structure, which is thought to reflect the differentiation of a magma chamber. The lower layers of the intrusion are composed of mafic rocks, while the upper layers are composed of felsic rocks. This layered structure provides valuable insights into the processes that occur during the formation of igneous rocks.

In conclusion, the Bushveld, Stillwater, and Skaergaard intrusions are three of the most significant layered igneous complexes in the world. They are known for their unique geological and petrological characteristics, as well as their economic importance. The study of these complexes provides valuable insights into the processes that occur during the formation of igneous rocks, and they are of great importance to the field of igneous petrology.




### Subsection: 3.8b Petrologic and geochemical features of these large layered intrusions

The Bushveld, Stillwater, and Skaergaard intrusions are not only significant for their size and economic importance, but also for their unique petrologic and geochemical features. These features provide valuable insights into the processes that formed these intrusions and the evolution of the Earth's mantle.

#### Bushveld Igneous Complex

The Bushveld Complex is characterized by a complex layering of different rock types, including ultramafic and mafic rocks. The lower sections of the complex are dominated by ultramafic rocks such as peridotite, chromitite, harzburgite, and bronzitite. These rocks are rich in iron and magnesium, and are thought to represent the earliest stages of the complex's formation.

As one moves upwards in the complex, the rock types become progressively more mafic, with the presence of norite, anorthosite, and gabbro. These rocks are richer in silicon and calcium, and are thought to represent later stages of the complex's formation. The complex also includes the felsic Rustenburg Layered Suite and the Lebowa Granite Suite, which are thought to represent the final stages of the complex's formation.

The Bushveld Complex is also characterized by its high platinum group metal (PGM) content. The PGM-bearing layers, or reefs, are of particular interest due to their economic importance. These reefs are thought to have formed as a result of the separation of different minerals during the complex's formation, with the PGM-rich minerals settling to the bottom of the intrusion.

#### Stillwater Igneous Complex

The Stillwater Complex, like the Bushveld Complex, is characterized by a complex layering of different rock types. The lower sections of the complex are dominated by ultramafic rocks such as peridotite and harzburgite. These rocks are thought to represent the earliest stages of the complex's formation.

As one moves upwards in the complex, the rock types become progressively more mafic, with the presence of norite, anorthosite, and gabbro. These rocks are thought to represent later stages of the complex's formation. The complex also includes the felsic Rustenburg Layered Suite and the Lebowa Granite Suite, which are thought to represent the final stages of the complex's formation.

The Stillwater Complex is also characterized by its high PGM content. The PGM-bearing layers, or reefs, are of particular interest due to their economic importance. These reefs are thought to have formed as a result of the separation of different minerals during the complex's formation, with the PGM-rich minerals settling to the bottom of the intrusion.

#### Skaergaard Intrusion

The Skaergaard Intrusion, located in Greenland, is characterized by a layering of different rock types, including ultramafic and mafic rocks. The lower sections of the intrusion are dominated by ultramafic rocks such as peridotite and harzburgite. These rocks are thought to represent the earliest stages of the intrusion's formation.

As one moves upwards in the intrusion, the rock types become progressively more mafic, with the presence of norite, anorthosite, and gabbro. These rocks are thought to represent later stages of the intrusion's formation. The intrusion also includes the felsic Rustenburg Layered Suite and the Lebowa Granite Suite, which are thought to represent the final stages of the intrusion's formation.

The Skaergaard Intrusion is also characterized by its high PGM content. The PGM-bearing layers, or reefs, are of particular interest due to their economic importance. These reefs are thought to have formed as a result of the separation of different minerals during the intrusion's formation, with the PGM-rich minerals settling to the bottom of the intrusion.




### Subsection: 3.9a Occurrence and petrogenesis of anorthosites

Anorthosites are a unique type of igneous rock that are of great interest to geologists due to their composition and formation. They are characterized by their high plagioclase feldspar content, with a minimal mafic component. This composition is not common in other types of igneous rocks, and it is still not fully understood how anorthosites form.

#### Occurrence of Anorthosites

Anorthosites can be found on Earth in five main types, with the first two being the most common. These two types have different modes of occurrence and are thought to have had different origins. Anorthosites are also found on the Moon, where they constitute the light-colored areas of the lunar surface.

On Earth, anorthosites typically occur as extensive stocks or batholiths. The areal extent of anorthosite batholiths can range from relatively small (dozens or hundreds of square kilometers) to nearly 100,000 square kilometers, as seen in the Nain Plutonic Suite or Mistastin crater in northern Labrador, Canada.

Major occurrences of anorthosite are found in the southeast U.S., the Appalachian Mountains, eastern Canada, across southern Scandinavia and eastern Europe, and in the Skaergaard intrusion in Greenland. Mapped onto the Pangaean continental configuration of that eon, these occurrences are all contained in a single straight belt, and must all have been emplaced intracratonally.

#### Petrogenesis of Anorthosites

The formation of anorthosites is still not fully understood, but most models involve the separation of plagioclase crystals based on their density. As plagioclase crystallizes in a magma chamber, the plagioclase crystals float to the top, concentrating there. This process is known as fractional crystallization.

However, the exact mechanisms and conditions that lead to the formation of anorthosites are still being researched. It is thought that the composition of the magma, the temperature and pressure conditions, and the presence of other minerals can all play a role in the formation of anorthosites.

In conclusion, anorthosites are a unique and fascinating type of igneous rock that have been the subject of much research. Their high plagioclase feldspar content and unusual mode of occurrence make them a valuable case study for understanding the processes that form igneous rocks. Further research and advancements in technology will continue to shed light on the significance of anorthosites in the field of igneous petrology.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the significance of igneous rock series in understanding the evolution of the Earth's crust. We have learned that igneous rocks are formed through the cooling and solidification of molten rock, and that they play a crucial role in shaping the Earth's surface. We have also discussed the different types of igneous rocks, including plutonic, volcanic, and extrusive igneous rocks, and how they are formed.

Furthermore, we have delved into the concept of rock series, which refers to a group of related igneous rocks that share similar mineral compositions and textures. We have seen how rock series can be used to trace the evolution of the Earth's crust, as different rock series can be found in different geological settings and at different stages of the Earth's history.

Overall, the study of igneous rock series is essential in understanding the complex processes that shape the Earth's crust. It allows us to piece together the puzzle of the Earth's history and gain a deeper understanding of the planet's past, present, and future.

## Chapter 4: Metamorphic Rocks




### Subsection: 3.9b Petrologic and geochemical characteristics of anorthosites

Anorthosites are characterized by their high plagioclase feldspar content, with a minimal mafic component. This composition is not common in other types of igneous rocks, and it is still not fully understood how anorthosites form. In this section, we will explore the petrologic and geochemical characteristics of anorthosites, including their chemical and isotopic characteristics.

#### Chemical and Isotopic Characteristics of Anorthosites

Anorthosites are typically >90% plagioclase, and the plagioclase composition is commonly between An<sub>40</sub> and An<sub>60</sub> (40–60% anorthite). This compositional range is intermediate, and is one of the characteristics which distinguish Proterozoic anorthosites from Archean anorthosites (which are typically >An<sub>80</sub>).

Proterozoic anorthosites often have significant mafic components in addition to plagioclase. These phases can include olivine, pyroxene, Fe-Ti oxides, and/or apatite. Mafic minerals in Proterozoic anorthosites have a wide range of composition, but are not generally highly magnesian.

The trace-element chemistry of Proterozoic anorthosites, and the associated rock types, has been examined in some detail by researchers with the aim of arriving at a plausible genetic theory. However, there is still little agreement on just what the results mean for anorthosite genesis; see the 'Origins' section below. A very short list of results, including results for rocks thought to be related to Proterozoic anorthosites,<Clarify|date=September 2013>

Some research has focused on neodymium (Nd) and strontium (Sr) isotopic determinations for anorthosites, particularly for anorthosites of the Nain Plutonic Suite (NPS). Such isotopic determinations are of use in gauging the viability of prospective sources for magmas that gave rise to anorthosites. Some results are detailed below in the 'Origins' section.

#### High-Alumina Orthopyroxene Megacrysts (HAOMs)

Many Proterozoic-age anorthosites contain large crystals of orthopyroxene with distinctive compositions. These are the so-called high-alumina orthopyroxene megacrysts (HAOM).

HAOM are distinctive because 1) they contain higher amounts of Al than typically seen in orthopyroxenes; 2) they are cut by numerous thin lathes of plagioclase, which may represent exsolution lamellae; and 3) they appear to be older than the anorthosites in which they occur. This suggests that HAOM may have crystallized from a separate magma source, and their presence in anorthosites adds complexity to the already intriguing story of anorthosite genesis.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the origins of anorthosites, exploring the various theories and models that have been proposed to explain their formation.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the significance of igneous rock series, exploring their formation, characteristics, and the role they play in the broader context of geology. We have seen how these rock series are not just random collections of rocks, but rather, they represent a systematic progression of magmatic processes and evolution. 

We have also learned about the importance of understanding these rock series in order to interpret the geological history of a region. By studying the composition and structure of these rocks, we can gain insights into the conditions under which they were formed, and the processes that shaped them. 

Furthermore, we have discussed the role of igneous rock series in the formation of other geological features, such as volcanic structures and mineral deposits. This understanding is crucial for mineral exploration and resource development. 

In conclusion, the study of igneous rock series is a fundamental aspect of geology. It provides a framework for understanding the complex processes that shape our planet, and it has practical applications in various fields, including mineral exploration, environmental management, and hazard assessment.

## Chapter 4: The Role of Igneous Processes in Ore Formation

### Introduction

The formation of ore is a complex process that involves a series of geological events. Among these, igneous processes play a crucial role. This chapter, "The Role of Igneous Processes in Ore Formation," delves into the intricate relationship between igneous processes and ore formation. 

Igneous processes are fundamental to the formation of many types of ore. These processes involve the crystallization of minerals from molten rock, or magma, which can result in the formation of ore deposits. The composition of the magma, as well as the conditions under which it crystallizes, can greatly influence the type and quality of ore that forms.

In this chapter, we will explore the various igneous processes involved in ore formation, including magma formation, differentiation, and crystallization. We will also discuss the role of these processes in the formation of different types of ore, such as copper, gold, and iron ore. 

Furthermore, we will examine the factors that influence the distribution and concentration of ore elements in igneous rocks. These factors include the composition of the magma, the temperature and pressure conditions during crystallization, and the presence of certain elements or compounds that can enhance ore formation.

By the end of this chapter, readers should have a solid understanding of the role of igneous processes in ore formation, and be able to apply this knowledge to interpret the formation of ore deposits in various geological settings. This chapter will provide a foundation for further exploration into the fascinating world of igneous petrology and ore genesis.




### Subsection: 3.10a Geology and igneous activity in lunar highland and maria regions

The Moon's surface is divided into two main regions: the highlands and the maria. The highlands, also known as terrae, are the older and higher regions of the Moon's surface, while the maria, or "seas," are the younger and lower regions. These regions have distinct geological and igneous characteristics that provide valuable insights into the Moon's history and evolution.

#### Geology of the Lunar Highlands

The lunar highlands are primarily composed of mafic plutonic rocks and regolith breccias. These rocks are dominantly ferroan anorthosites, which are characterized by their high plagioclase feldspar content. The ferroan anorthosites are further divided into the ferroan anorthosite suite, the magnesian suite, and the alkali suite.

The ferroan anorthosite suite is the most common type of anorthosite in the highlands. It is characterized by its high iron content, with an average FeO content of 18–21%. The magnesian suite, on the other hand, has a lower iron content, with an average FeO content of 14–16%. The alkali suite is the least common type of anorthosite, with an average FeO content of 12–14%.

#### Igneous Activity in the Lunar Highlands

The highlands of the Moon are believed to have formed through a series of igneous processes. The most likely scenario is that the Moon's mantle was differentiated into a ferroan and magnesian layer, which then rose to the surface and formed the highlands. This process is supported by the chemical and isotopic characteristics of the highland rocks.

The highlands also exhibit evidence of impact melting, which is thought to have occurred during the formation of the Moon's large impact basins. This melting resulted in the formation of breccias, which are dominantly composed of highland lithologies. These breccias provide valuable insights into the composition and evolution of the highlands.

#### Geology of the Lunar Marias

The maria, or "seas," of the Moon are primarily composed of mare basalts. These basalts are named as such because they frequently constitute large portions of the lunar maria. They are typically characterized by their high iron content, with an average FeO content of 18–21%.

The mare basalts are also known for their high titanium content, with some basalts containing up to 13% TiO<sub>2</sub>. This high titanium content is thought to be due to the presence of ilmenite, a titanium-rich mineral, in the basalts.

#### Igneous Activity in the Lunar Marias

The maria of the Moon are believed to have formed through a series of volcanic processes. The most likely scenario is that the Moon's mantle was differentiated into a ferroan and magnesian layer, which then rose to the surface and formed the maria. This process is supported by the chemical and isotopic characteristics of the maria rocks.

The maria also exhibit evidence of impact melting, which is thought to have occurred during the formation of the Moon's large impact basins. This melting resulted in the formation of basaltic lavas, which are dominantly composed of mare basalts. These lavas provide valuable insights into the composition and evolution of the maria.

In conclusion, the geology and igneous activity of the lunar highlands and maria regions provide valuable insights into the Moon's history and evolution. The distinct characteristics of these regions highlight the complex processes that have shaped the Moon's surface over billions of years.




### Subsection: 3.10b Petrologic and geochemical features of lunar igneous rocks

The petrologic and geochemical features of lunar igneous rocks provide valuable insights into the Moon's history and evolution. These rocks are primarily composed of olivine, pyroxene, and plagioclase feldspar, with some rocks also containing ilmenite and a new mineral named armalcolite.

#### Petrologic Features of Lunar Igneous Rocks

The petrologic features of lunar igneous rocks are largely similar to those found on Earth, with the main difference being the higher abundance of iron and magnesium in lunar rocks. This is particularly evident in the basaltic rocks of the maria, which contain higher abundances of olivine and pyroxene compared to the anorthositic rocks of the highlands.

The basaltic rocks of the maria are also richer in iron than terrestrial basalts, and have lower viscosities. This is due to the higher iron content, which lowers the melting point of the rocks and allows them to flow more easily. The lower viscosity also allows these rocks to spread out and form the large, flat plains that characterize the maria.

#### Geochemical Features of Lunar Igneous Rocks

The geochemical features of lunar igneous rocks are also unique and provide valuable insights into the Moon's history. The maria, for example, are composed predominantly of basalt, which is rich in iron and magnesium. This is in contrast to the highlands, which are composed primarily of anorthosite, a rock composed primarily of calcium-rich plagioclase feldspar.

The composition of the maria also varies significantly, with some regions containing higher abundances of olivine and pyroxene, while others contain more plagioclase. This variation is thought to be due to the different sources of magma that formed the maria, as well as the influence of the Moon's early atmosphere.

#### The Role of KREEP in Lunar Igneous Rocks

The KREEP (potassium, rare-earth elements, and phosphorus) component is also a significant component of the lunar crust. It is thought to be related to the petrogenesis of KREEP, and is found in the igneous "Mg-suite" rocks, such as the troctolites, norites, and KREEP-basalts.

The KREEP component is particularly important in understanding the formation of the Moon's highlands. It is thought to have been derived from the Moon's early atmosphere, which was rich in potassium and other rare-earth elements. This component is also thought to have played a role in the formation of the Moon's large impact basins, as it is found in the breccias that make up the regolith of these basins.

In conclusion, the petrologic and geochemical features of lunar igneous rocks provide valuable insights into the Moon's history and evolution. The unique composition and characteristics of these rocks continue to be a topic of research and study, as scientists strive to unravel the mysteries of the Moon's early history.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the significance of igneous rock series in understanding the evolution of the Earth's crust. We have learned that igneous rocks are formed through the cooling and solidification of molten rock, and that they play a crucial role in shaping the Earth's surface. We have also discussed the different types of igneous rocks, including plutonic and volcanic rocks, and how they are formed.

Furthermore, we have delved into the concept of rock series, which refers to a group of related igneous rocks that share similar mineral compositions and textures. We have seen how rock series can be used to trace the evolution of the Earth's crust, as different rock series are formed at different stages of the Earth's history. This understanding is crucial in the field of geology, as it allows us to reconstruct the past and predict future changes in the Earth's crust.

In conclusion, the study of igneous rock series is essential in understanding the dynamic nature of the Earth's crust. It provides us with valuable insights into the processes that shape our planet and helps us to better understand the world around us.

## Chapter: Advanced Igneous Petrology Textbook

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of metamorphic rocks. Metamorphic rocks are formed when igneous or sedimentary rocks are subjected to high temperatures and pressures, causing them to change in mineral composition and texture. This process is known as metamorphism and it plays a crucial role in the evolution of the Earth's crust.

We will begin by exploring the different types of metamorphic rocks, including gneiss, schist, and marble. Each of these rocks has its own unique characteristics and formation processes, and understanding them is essential for any advanced study of igneous petrology.

Next, we will discuss the various factors that influence metamorphism, such as temperature, pressure, and the composition of the original rock. We will also examine the different types of metamorphic reactions that occur, including recrystallization, phase changes, and the formation of new minerals.

Finally, we will look at the role of metamorphic rocks in the formation of mountains and other geological features. We will also discuss the economic importance of metamorphic rocks, as they are often used in construction and industry.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a comprehensive understanding of metamorphic rocks and their significance in the study of igneous petrology. So let's dive in and explore the fascinating world of metamorphic rocks.


# Advanced Igneous Petrology Textbook

## Chapter 4: Metamorphic Rocks




### Subsection: 3.11a Classification and petrogenesis of Howardites, Eucrites, and Diogenites

The Howardites, Eucrites, and Diogenites (HED) are a group of primitive achondrite meteorites that have been extensively studied due to their unique composition and petrologic features. These meteorites are believed to originate from the Moon, and their study has provided valuable insights into the early history of our solar system.

#### Classification of HED Meteorites

The HED meteorites are classified based on their mineral composition and texture. Howardites are fine-grained, basaltic rocks that are rich in iron and magnesium. Eucrites, on the other hand, are coarse-grained, plagioclase-rich rocks that are less iron-rich than basaltic rocks. Diogenites are intermediate in composition between howardites and eucrites, and are characterized by their high iron and magnesium content.

The classification of HED meteorites is important as it helps us understand the processes that formed these rocks. The differences in mineral composition and texture suggest that these rocks were formed under different conditions, and their study can provide insights into the early history of the Moon.

#### Petrogenesis of HED Meteorites

The petrogenesis of HED meteorites is a topic of ongoing research. The current understanding is that these rocks were formed by the melting of the Moon's early crust, which was composed of anorthosite. This melting was likely caused by impacts from asteroids and comets, which were common in the early solar system.

The melting of the Moon's early crust resulted in the formation of magma, which then differentiated into different types of rocks based on their mineral composition and texture. The basaltic rocks, such as howardites and eucrites, were formed from the melting of the lower crust, while the plagioclase-rich rocks, such as diogenites, were formed from the melting of the upper crust.

#### The Role of KREEP in HED Meteorites

The KREEP (potassium, rare-earth elements, and phosphorus) component is also a significant component of HED meteorites. It is believed to originate from the Moon's early mantle, and its presence in HED meteorites suggests that these rocks were formed from the melting of the Moon's early mantle.

The KREEP component is particularly important in the petrogenesis of HED meteorites as it provides insights into the early history of the Moon. Its presence suggests that the Moon's early mantle was rich in potassium, rare-earth elements, and phosphorus, which can be used to reconstruct the early conditions on the Moon.

In conclusion, the study of HED meteorites, particularly their classification and petrogenesis, is crucial in understanding the early history of the Moon and the solar system. The unique composition and petrologic features of these meteorites continue to be a topic of ongoing research, and their study will continue to provide valuable insights into the early history of our solar system.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the significance of igneous rock series in understanding the evolution of the Earth's crust. We have learned that igneous rocks are formed through the cooling and solidification of molten rock, and that they can provide valuable information about the processes that shape our planet. By studying the different types of igneous rocks, we can gain insights into the composition and history of the Earth's crust.

We have also discussed the importance of understanding the petrology of igneous rocks, which is the study of their mineral composition and texture. This knowledge can help us identify the source of the rocks and the conditions under which they were formed. By studying the petrology of igneous rocks, we can better understand the processes that drive plate tectonics and the movement of the Earth's crust.

Furthermore, we have examined the role of igneous rocks in the formation of different types of landscapes. From volcanic mountains to granite domes, igneous rocks play a crucial role in shaping the Earth's surface. By studying the properties of these rocks, we can gain a deeper understanding of the geological processes that have shaped our planet over billions of years.

In conclusion, the study of igneous rock series is essential for understanding the Earth's crust and its evolution. By examining the different types of igneous rocks and their petrology, we can gain valuable insights into the processes that shape our planet. This knowledge is crucial for geologists and other scientists who study the Earth's crust and its history.

## Chapter: Advanced Igneous Petrology Textbook

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of meteorite petrology. Meteorites are small, solid objects that originate from outer space and fall to Earth. They provide valuable insights into the composition and processes of the early solar system. By studying the petrology of meteorites, we can gain a better understanding of the formation and evolution of the Earth and other planets.

We will begin by discussing the different types of meteorites and their unique characteristics. We will then explore the various techniques used to analyze and classify meteorites, including spectroscopy, microscopy, and chemical analysis. We will also examine the role of meteorites in the study of the early solar system, including their potential as samples of the Moon, Mars, and other bodies.

Next, we will delve into the petrology of different types of meteorites, including iron, stony-iron, and stony meteorites. We will discuss the mineral composition and texture of these meteorites, as well as their formation processes. We will also explore the role of meteorites in the formation of the Earth's early atmosphere and oceans.

Finally, we will examine the impact of meteorites on the Earth's surface and the formation of impact craters. We will also discuss the potential for meteorite impacts to have played a role in the extinction of dinosaurs and other species. By the end of this chapter, you will have a comprehensive understanding of meteorite petrology and its importance in the study of the early solar system.


# Advanced Igneous Petrology Textbook

## Chapter 4: Meteorite Petrology

 4.1: Meteorite Classification and Composition

Meteorites are small, solid objects that originate from outer space and fall to Earth. They provide valuable insights into the composition and processes of the early solar system. By studying the petrology of meteorites, we can gain a better understanding of the formation and evolution of the Earth and other planets.

### Subsection 4.1a: Classification of Meteorites

Meteorites can be classified into three main categories based on their composition: iron, stony-iron, and stony meteorites. Iron meteorites are composed primarily of iron and nickel, while stony-iron meteorites have a higher proportion of silicate minerals. Stony meteorites, on the other hand, are composed primarily of silicate minerals and have very little iron and nickel.

The classification of meteorites is based on their mineral composition and texture, as well as their spectral properties. Spectroscopy is a powerful tool for classifying meteorites, as it allows us to determine the relative abundance of different elements in a sample. This information can then be compared to known spectra of different types of meteorites to determine their classification.

Microscopy is another important technique for studying meteorites. By examining the mineral composition and texture of a meteorite under a microscope, we can gain valuable insights into its formation processes. This is particularly useful for stony meteorites, which can provide information about the early solar system and the formation of the Earth.

Chemical analysis is also used to classify meteorites. By analyzing the chemical composition of a meteorite, we can determine its origin and potential as a sample of the early solar system. This information can then be used to further our understanding of the formation and evolution of the Earth and other planets.

### Subsection 4.1b: Composition of Meteorites

The composition of meteorites can vary greatly depending on their classification. Iron meteorites, for example, are composed primarily of iron and nickel, with trace amounts of other elements. Stony-iron meteorites, on the other hand, have a higher proportion of silicate minerals, with iron and nickel making up a smaller percentage.

Stony meteorites are composed primarily of silicate minerals, with very little iron and nickel. The mineral composition of stony meteorites can vary greatly, providing valuable insights into the early solar system and the formation of the Earth. Some stony meteorites, such as chondrites, are believed to be samples of the early solar system and can provide information about the composition of the early Earth.

### Subsection 4.1c: Role of Meteorites in Early Solar System

Meteorites play a crucial role in our understanding of the early solar system. By studying the composition and formation processes of meteorites, we can gain insights into the early Earth and other planets. Meteorites can also provide samples of the early solar system, allowing us to study the composition and processes of the early Earth and other planets.

One of the most significant contributions of meteorites to our understanding of the early solar system is their potential as samples of the Moon, Mars, and other bodies. By studying the composition and formation processes of meteorites, we can gain insights into the composition and processes of these bodies, providing valuable information about their early history.

In addition to their role in studying the early solar system, meteorites also play a crucial role in the study of impact cratering. By examining the impact craters on the Earth and other planets, we can gain insights into the size and composition of the objects that caused these impacts. This information can then be used to further our understanding of the early solar system and the formation of the Earth.

In conclusion, the study of meteorite petrology is essential for our understanding of the early solar system and the formation of the Earth. By studying the composition and formation processes of meteorites, we can gain valuable insights into the early Earth and other planets, providing a deeper understanding of our place in the universe.
